<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_chromium" title="Template:Infobox chromium"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_chromium" title="Template talk:Infobox chromium"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_chromium&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div> | <a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Chromium</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with symbol <b>Cr</b> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 24. It is the first element in <a href="/wiki/Group_6_element" title="Group 6 element">group 6</a>. It is a steely-grey, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luster_(mineralogy)" title="Luster (mineralogy)">lustrous</a>, hard and brittle <a href="/wiki/Transition_metal" title="Transition metal">transition metal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Chromium boasts a high usage rate as a <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metal</a> that is able to be highly <a href="/wiki/Polishing" title="Polishing">polished</a> while resisting <a href="/wiki/Tarnish" title="Tarnish">tarnishing</a>. Chromium is also the main component of <a href="/wiki/Stainless_steel" title="Stainless steel">stainless steel</a>, a popular steel alloy due to its uncommonly high <a href="/wiki/Specular_reflection" title="Specular reflection">specular reflection</a>. Simple polished chromium reflects almost 70% of the <a href="/wiki/Visible_spectrum" title="Visible spectrum">visible spectrum</a>, with almost 90% of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infrared_light" title="Infrared light">infrared light</a> waves being reflected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The name of the element is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> word χρῶμα, <i>chrōma</i>, meaning <a href="/wiki/Color" title="Color">color</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> because many chromium compounds are intensely colored.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferrochromium" title="Ferrochromium">Ferrochromium</a> alloy is commercially produced from <a href="/wiki/Chromite" title="Chromite">chromite</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silicothermic" title="Silicothermic">silicothermic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Aluminothermic_reaction" title="Aluminothermic reaction">aluminothermic reactions</a> and chromium metal by <a href="/wiki/Roasting_(metallurgy)" title="Roasting (metallurgy)">roasting</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leaching_(metallurgy)" title="Leaching (metallurgy)">leaching</a> processes followed by reduction with <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> and then <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a>. Chromium metal is of high value for its high <a href="/wiki/Corrosion" title="Corrosion">corrosion</a> resistance and <a href="/wiki/Hardness" title="Hardness">hardness</a>. A major development in steel production was the discovery that steel could be made highly resistant to corrosion and discoloration by adding metallic chromium to form <a href="/wiki/Stainless_steel" title="Stainless steel">stainless steel</a>. Stainless steel and <a href="/wiki/Chrome_plating" title="Chrome plating">chrome plating</a> (<a href="/wiki/Electroplating" title="Electroplating">electroplating</a> with chromium) together comprise 85% of the commercial use.
</p><p>In the United States, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trivalent" title="Trivalent">trivalent</a> chromium (Cr(III)) <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ion</a> is considered an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Essential_nutrient" title="Essential nutrient">essential nutrient</a> in humans for <a href="/wiki/Insulin" title="Insulin">insulin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipid</a> <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ods_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-ods-7">[7]</a></sup> However, in 2014, the <a href="/wiki/European_Food_Safety_Authority" title="European Food Safety Authority">European Food Safety Authority</a>, acting for the European Union, concluded that there was not sufficient evidence for chromium to be recognized as essential.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>While chromium metal and Cr(III) ions are not considered toxic, <a href="/wiki/Hexavalent_chromium" title="Hexavalent chromium">hexavalent chromium</a> (Cr(VI)) is both toxic and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carcinogenic" title="Carcinogenic">carcinogenic</a>. Abandoned chromium production sites often require <a href="/wiki/Environmental_remediation" title="Environmental remediation">environmental cleanup</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_properties">Physical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Physical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Chromium is remarkable for its magnetic properties: it is the only elemental solid which shows <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antiferromagnetic" title="Antiferromagnetic">antiferromagnetic</a> ordering at room temperature (and below). Above 38 °C, it changes to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paramagnetic" title="Paramagnetic">paramagnetic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fawcett_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-fawcett-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Isotopes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_chromium" title="Isotopes of chromium">Isotopes of chromium</a></div>
<p>Naturally occurring chromium is composed of three stable <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a>; <sup>52</sup>Cr, <sup>53</sup>Cr and <sup>54</sup>Cr, with <sup>52</sup>Cr being the most abundant (83.789% <a href="/wiki/Natural_abundance" title="Natural abundance">natural abundance</a>). 19 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioisotope" title="Radioisotope">radioisotopes</a> have been characterized, with the most stable being <sup>50</sup>Cr with a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of (more than) 1.8<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">17</span></sup> years, and <sup>51</sup>Cr with a half-life of 27.7 days. All of the remaining <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactive" title="Radioactive">radioactive</a> isotopes have half-lives that are less than 24 hours and the majority less than 1 minute. This element also has 2 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meta_state" title="Meta state">meta states</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p><sup>53</sup>Cr is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiogenic" title="Radiogenic">radiogenic</a> decay product of <sup>53</sup><a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">Mn</a> (half-life = 3.74 million years).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Chromium isotopes are typically collocated (and compounded) with <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a> isotopes. This circumstance is useful in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isotope_geology" title="Isotope geology">isotope geology</a>. Manganese-chromium isotope ratios reinforce the evidence from <sup>26</sup><a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">Al</a> and <sup>107</sup><a href="/wiki/Palladium" title="Palladium">Pd</a> concerning the early history of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_system" title="Solar system">solar system</a>. Variations in <sup>53</sup>Cr/<sup>52</sup>Cr and Mn/Cr ratios from several meteorites indicate an initial <sup>53</sup>Mn/<sup>55</sup>Mn ratio that suggests Mn-Cr isotopic composition must result from in-situ decay of <sup>53</sup>Mn in differentiated planetary bodies. Hence <sup>53</sup>Cr provides additional evidence for <a href="/wiki/Nucleosynthesis" title="Nucleosynthesis">nucleosynthetic</a> processes immediately before coalescence of the solar system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53Mn53Cr_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-53Mn53Cr-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>The isotopes of chromium range in <a href="/wiki/Atomic_mass" title="Atomic mass">atomic mass</a> from 43 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_mass_unit" title="Atomic mass unit">u</a> (<sup>43</sup>Cr) to 67 u (<sup>67</sup>Cr). The primary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decay_mode" title="Decay mode">decay mode</a> before the most abundant stable isotope, <sup>52</sup>Cr, is <a href="/wiki/Electron_capture" title="Electron capture">electron capture</a> and the primary mode after is <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE-9">[9]</a></sup> <sup>53</sup>Cr has been posited as a proxy for atmospheric oxygen concentration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry_and_compounds">Chemistry and compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Chemistry and compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Chromium_compounds" title="Category:Chromium compounds">Category:Chromium compounds</a>.</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_properties">Chemical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Chemical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chromium_in_water_pourbiax_diagram.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="498" data-file-width="500" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c3/Chromium_in_water_pourbiax_diagram.png/220px-Chromium_in_water_pourbiax_diagram.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c3/Chromium_in_water_pourbiax_diagram.png/330px-Chromium_in_water_pourbiax_diagram.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c3/Chromium_in_water_pourbiax_diagram.png/440px-Chromium_in_water_pourbiax_diagram.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chromium_in_water_pourbiax_diagram.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Pourbaix_diagram" title="Pourbaix diagram">Pourbaix diagram</a> for chromium in pure water, perchloric acid, or sodium hydroxide<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crspeci_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crspeci-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-medusa_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-medusa-14">[14]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Chromium is a member of <a href="/wiki/Group_6_element" title="Group 6 element">group 6</a>, of the <a href="/wiki/Transition_metal" title="Transition metal">transition metals</a>. Chromium(0) has an electron configuration of [Ar]3d<sup>5</sup>4s<sup>1</sup>, owing to the lower energy of the <a href="/wiki/Spin_states_(d_electrons)" title="Spin states (d electrons)">high spin configuration</a>. Chromium exhibits a wide range of <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation states</a>, but chromium being ionized into a cation with a positive 3 charge serves as chromium's most stable ionic state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> The +3 and +6 states occur the most commonly within chromium compounds; charges of +1, +4 and +5 for chromium are rare, but nevertheless due occasionally exist for chromium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Chromium_passivation">Chromium passivation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Chromium passivation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Chromium metal left standing in air is <a href="/wiki/Passivation_(chemistry)" title="Passivation (chemistry)">passivated</a> by oxidation, forming a thin, protective, surface layer. This layer is a <a href="/wiki/Spinel" title="Spinel">spinel</a> structure only a few molecules thick. It is very dense, and prevents the diffusion of oxygen into the underlying metal. This is different from the oxide that forms on iron and carbon steel, through which elemental oxygen continues to migrate, reaching the underlying material to cause incessant <a href="/wiki/Rust" title="Rust">rusting</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Passivation can be enhanced by short contact with <a href="/wiki/Oxidizing_acid" title="Oxidizing acid">oxidizing acids</a> like <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a>. Passivated chromium is stable against acids. Passivation can be removed with a strong <a href="/wiki/Reducing_agent" title="Reducing agent">reducing agent</a> that destroys the protective oxide layer on the metal. Chromium metal treated in this way readily dissolves in weak acids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HollemanAF_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-HollemanAF-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chromium, unlike such metals as iron and nickel, does not suffer from <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_embrittlement" title="Hydrogen embrittlement">hydrogen embrittlement</a>. However, it does suffer from nitrogen <a href="/wiki/Embrittlement" title="Embrittlement">embrittlement</a>, reacting with nitrogen from air and forming brittle nitrides at the high temperatures necessary to work the metal parts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>

<h4><span id="Chromium.28III.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chromium(III)">Chromium(III)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Chromium(III)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chlorid_chromit%C3%BD.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="511" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Chlorid_chromit%C3%BD.JPG/170px-Chlorid_chromit%C3%BD.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Chlorid_chromit%C3%BD.JPG/255px-Chlorid_chromit%C3%BD.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Chlorid_chromit%C3%BD.JPG/340px-Chlorid_chromit%C3%BD.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chlorid_chromit%C3%BD.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chromium(III) chloride hexahydrate ([CrCl<sub>2</sub>(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>4</sub>]Cl·2H<sub>2</sub>O)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chromium(III)-chloride-purple-anhydrous-sunlight.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="693" data-file-width="927" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Chromium%28III%29-chloride-purple-anhydrous-sunlight.jpg/170px-Chromium%28III%29-chloride-purple-anhydrous-sunlight.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Chromium%28III%29-chloride-purple-anhydrous-sunlight.jpg/255px-Chromium%28III%29-chloride-purple-anhydrous-sunlight.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Chromium%28III%29-chloride-purple-anhydrous-sunlight.jpg/340px-Chromium%28III%29-chloride-purple-anhydrous-sunlight.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chromium(III)-chloride-purple-anhydrous-sunlight.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Anhydrous chromium(III) chloride (CrCl<sub>3</sub>)</div></div></div>
<p>A large number of chromium(III) compounds are known; chromium(III) can be obtained by dissolving elemental chromium in acids like <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a>. The <span class="chemf nowrap">Cr<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> ion has a similar radius (63 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Picometer" title="Picometer">pm</a>) to <span class="chemf nowrap">Al<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> (radius 50 pm), and they can replace each other in some compounds, such as in <a href="/wiki/Chrome_alum" title="Chrome alum">chrome alum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alum" title="Alum">alum</a>. When a trace amount of <span class="chemf nowrap">Cr<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> replaces <span class="chemf nowrap">Al<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> in <a href="/wiki/Corundum" title="Corundum">corundum</a> (aluminium oxide, Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pink_sapphire" title="Pink sapphire">pink sapphire</a> or red-colored <a href="/wiki/Ruby" title="Ruby">ruby</a> is formed, depending on the amount of chromium.
</p><p>Chromium(III) ions tend to form <a href="/wiki/Octahedral_molecular_geometry" title="Octahedral molecular geometry">octahedral</a> complexes.  The color of these complexes is determined by the ligands attached to the Cr center.  Commercially available <a href="/wiki/Chromium(III)_chloride" title="Chromium(III) chloride">chromium(III) chloride</a> hydrate is the dark green complex [CrCl<sub>2</sub>(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>4</sub>]Cl.  Closely related compounds have different colors: pale green [CrCl(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>5</sub>]Cl<sub>2</sub> and violet [Cr(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>6</sub>]Cl<sub>3</sub>. If water-free green <a href="/wiki/Chromium(III)_chloride" title="Chromium(III) chloride">chromium(III) chloride</a> is dissolved in water, the green solution turns violet after some time as the chloride in the inner <a href="/wiki/Coordination_sphere" title="Coordination sphere">coordination sphere</a> is replaced by water. This kind of reaction is also observed with solutions of <a href="/wiki/Chrome_alum" title="Chrome alum">chrome alum</a> and other water-soluble chromium(III) salts.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Chromium(III)_hydroxide" title="Chromium(III) hydroxide">Chromium(III) hydroxide</a> (Cr(OH)<sub>3</sub>) is <a href="/wiki/Amphoterism" title="Amphoterism">amphoteric</a>, dissolving in acidic solutions to form [Cr(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>3+</sup>, and in basic solutions to form <span class="chemf nowrap">[Cr(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>]<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>. It is dehydrated by heating to form the green <a href="/wiki/Chromium(III)_oxide" title="Chromium(III) oxide">chromium(III) oxide</a> (Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>), a stable oxide with a crystal structure identical to that of <a href="/wiki/Corundum" title="Corundum">corundum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HollemanAF_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-HollemanAF-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Chromium.28VI.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chromium(VI)">Chromium(VI)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Chromium(VI)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chrom(VI)-oxid.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="840" data-file-width="1120" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Chrom%28VI%29-oxid.jpg/170px-Chrom%28VI%29-oxid.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Chrom%28VI%29-oxid.jpg/255px-Chrom%28VI%29-oxid.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Chrom%28VI%29-oxid.jpg/340px-Chrom%28VI%29-oxid.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chrom(VI)-oxid.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chromium(VI) oxide</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hexavalent_chromium" title="Hexavalent chromium">Chromium(VI) compounds</a> are powerful oxidants at low or neutral pH.  Most important are <a href="/wiki/Chromate_and_dichromate" title="Chromate and dichromate">chromate</a> anion (<span class="chemf nowrap">CrO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>) and <a href="/wiki/Chromate_and_dichromate" title="Chromate and dichromate">dichromate</a> (Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub><sup>2−</sup>) anions, which exist in equilibrium:
</p>
<dl><dd>2 [CrO<sub>4</sub>]<sup>2−</sup>  +  2 H<sup>+</sup>  ⇌ [Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>]<sup>2−</sup>  +  H<sub>2</sub>O</dd></dl>
<p>Chromium(VI) halides are known also and include the <a href="/wiki/Hexafluoride" title="Hexafluoride">hexafluoride</a> <a href="/wiki/Chromium_hexafluoride" title="Chromium hexafluoride">CrF<sub>6</sub></a> and <a href="/wiki/Chromyl_chloride" title="Chromyl chloride">chromyl chloride</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">CrO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>Cl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HollemanAF_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-HollemanAF-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sodium_chromate" title="Sodium chromate">Sodium chromate</a> is produced industrially by the oxidative roasting of <a href="/wiki/Chromite" title="Chromite">chromite</a> ore with <a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">calcium</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sodium_carbonate" title="Sodium carbonate">sodium carbonate</a>. The dominant species is therefore, by the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_mass_action" title="Law of mass action">law of mass action</a>, determined by the pH of the solution. The change in equilibrium is visible by a change from yellow (chromate) to orange (dichromate), such as when an acid is added to a neutral solution of <a href="/wiki/Potassium_chromate" title="Potassium chromate">potassium chromate</a>. At yet lower pH values, further condensation to more complex <a href="/wiki/Oxyanion" title="Oxyanion">oxyanions</a> of chromium is possible.
</p><p>Both the <a href="/wiki/Chromate_and_dichromate" title="Chromate and dichromate">chromate and dichromate</a> anions are strong oxidizing reagents at low pH:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HollemanAF_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-HollemanAF-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chroman_sodn%C3%BD.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="405" data-file-width="640" height="108" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Chroman_sodn%C3%BD.JPG/170px-Chroman_sodn%C3%BD.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Chroman_sodn%C3%BD.JPG/255px-Chroman_sodn%C3%BD.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Chroman_sodn%C3%BD.JPG/340px-Chroman_sodn%C3%BD.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chroman_sodn%C3%BD.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sodium_chromate" title="Sodium chromate">Sodium chromate</a> (Na<sub>2</sub>CrO<sub>4</sub>)</div></div></div>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">Cr<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">7</sub></span></span> + 14 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + 6 e<sup>−</sup> → 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">Cr<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + 21 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> (ε<sub>0</sub> = 1.33 V)</dd></dl>
<p>They are, however, only moderately oxidizing at high pH:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HollemanAF_18-4"><a href="#cite_note-HollemanAF-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">CrO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> + 4 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> + 3 e<sup>−</sup> → <span class="chemf nowrap">Cr(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> + 5 <span class="chemf nowrap">OH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> (ε<sub>0</sub> = −0.13 V)</dd></dl>
<p>Chromium(VI) compounds in solution can be detected by adding an acidic <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_peroxide" title="Hydrogen peroxide">hydrogen peroxide</a> solution. The unstable dark blue <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chromium(VI)_peroxide" title="Chromium(VI) peroxide">chromium(VI) peroxide</a> (CrO<sub>5</sub>) is formed, which can be stabilized as an ether adduct <span class="chemf nowrap">CrO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span>·OR<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HollemanAF_18-5"><a href="#cite_note-HollemanAF-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Chromic_acid" title="Chromic acid">Chromic acid</a> has the hypothetical formula <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>CrO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>. It is a vaguely described chemical, despite many well-defined chromates and dichromates being known.  The dark red <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chromium(VI)_oxide" title="Chromium(VI) oxide">chromium(VI) oxide</a> <span class="chemf nowrap">CrO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>, the acid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anhydride" title="Anhydride">anhydride</a> of chromic acid, is sold industrially as "chromic acid".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HollemanAF_18-6"><a href="#cite_note-HollemanAF-18">[18]</a></sup> It can be produced by mixing sulfuric acid with dichromate, and is a strong oxidizing agent.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_oxidation_states">Other oxidation states</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Other oxidation states">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span id="Chromium.28V.29_and_chromium.28IV.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chromium(V)_and_chromium(IV)">Chromium(V) and chromium(IV)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Chromium(V) and chromium(IV)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The oxidation state +5 is only realized in few compounds but are intermediates in many reactions involving oxidations by chromate. The only binary compound is the volatile <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chromium(V)_fluoride" title="Chromium(V) fluoride">chromium(V) fluoride</a> (CrF<sub>5</sub>). This red solid has a melting point of 30 °C and a boiling point of 117 °C. It can be prepared by treating chromium metal with fluorine at 400 °C and 200 bar pressure. The peroxochromate(V) is another example of the +5 oxidation state. <a href="/wiki/Potassium_tetraperoxochromate(V)" title="Potassium tetraperoxochromate(V)">Potassium peroxochromate</a> (K<sub>3</sub>[Cr(O<sub>2</sub>)<sub>4</sub>]) is made by reacting potassium chromate with hydrogen peroxide at low temperatures. This red brown compound is stable at room temperature but decomposes spontaneously at 150–170 °C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>Compounds of chromium(IV) (in the +4 oxidation state) are slightly more common than those of chromium(V). The tetrahalides, <a href="/wiki/Chromium(IV)_fluoride" title="Chromium(IV) fluoride">CrF<sub>4</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/Chromium(IV)_chloride" title="Chromium(IV) chloride">CrCl<sub>4</sub></a>, and CrBr<sub>4</sub>, can be produced by treating the trihalides (<span class="chemf nowrap">CrX<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>) with the corresponding halogen at elevated temperatures. Such compounds are susceptible to disproportionation reactions and are not stable in water.
</p>
<h4><span id="Chromium.28II.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chromium(II)">Chromium(II)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Chromium(II)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Many chromium(II) compounds are known, such as the water-stable <a href="/wiki/Chromium(II)_chloride" title="Chromium(II) chloride">chromium(II) chloride</a> <span class="chemf nowrap">CrCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> that can be made by reducing chromium(III) chloride with zinc. The resulting bright blue solution created from dissolving chromium(II) chloride is only stable at neutral <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HollemanAF_18-7"><a href="#cite_note-HollemanAF-18">[18]</a></sup> Some other notable chromium(II) compounds include <a href="/wiki/Chromium(II)_oxide" title="Chromium(II) oxide">chromium(II) oxide</a> <span class="chemf nowrap">CrO</span>, and <a href="/wiki/Chromium(II)_sulfate" title="Chromium(II) sulfate">chromium(II) sulfate</a> <span class="chemf nowrap">CrSO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>. Many chromous carboxylates are known as well, the most famous of these being the red <a href="/wiki/Chromium(II)_acetate" title="Chromium(II) acetate">chromium(II) acetate</a> (Cr<sub>2</sub>(O<sub>2</sub>CCH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>4</sub>) that features a quadruple bond.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Chromium.28I.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chromium(I)">Chromium(I)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Chromium(I)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Most chromium(I) compounds are obtained solely by oxidation of electron-rich, <a href="/wiki/Octahedral_molecular_geometry" title="Octahedral molecular geometry">octahedral</a> chromium(0) complexes. Other chromium(I) complexes contain <a href="/wiki/Cyclopentadienyl" title="Cyclopentadienyl">cyclopentadienyl</a> ligands. As verified by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-ray_diffraction" title="X-ray diffraction">X-ray diffraction</a>, a Cr-Cr <a href="/wiki/Quintuple_bond" title="Quintuple bond">quintuple bond</a> (length 183.51(4)  pm) has also been described.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup> Extremely bulky monodentate ligands stabilize this compound by shielding the quintuple bond from further reactions.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:5-fold_chromium.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1178" data-file-width="1060" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/5-fold_chromium.png/220px-5-fold_chromium.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/5-fold_chromium.png/330px-5-fold_chromium.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/5-fold_chromium.png/440px-5-fold_chromium.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:5-fold_chromium.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chromium compound determined experimentally to contain a Cr-Cr quintuple bond</div></div></div>
<h4><span id="Chromium.280.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chromium(0)">Chromium(0)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Chromium(0)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Organochromium_chemistry" title="Organochromium chemistry">Organochromium chemistry</a></div>
<p>Many chromium(0) compounds are currently known; however, most of these compounds are derivatives of the compounds <a href="/wiki/Chromium_hexacarbonyl" title="Chromium hexacarbonyl">chromium hexacarbonyl</a> or <a href="/wiki/Bis(benzene)chromium" title="Bis(benzene)chromium">bis(benzene)chromium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Occurrence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Chromium_minerals" title="Category:Chromium minerals">Category:Chromium minerals</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Crocoite_from_Tasmania.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Crocoite_from_Tasmania.jpg/170px-Crocoite_from_Tasmania.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Crocoite_from_Tasmania.jpg/255px-Crocoite_from_Tasmania.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Crocoite_from_Tasmania.jpg/340px-Crocoite_from_Tasmania.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Crocoite_from_Tasmania.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Crocoite" title="Crocoite">Crocoite</a> (PbCrO<sub>4</sub>)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chromit_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1469" data-file-width="1837" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Chromit_1.jpg/170px-Chromit_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Chromit_1.jpg/255px-Chromit_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Chromit_1.jpg/340px-Chromit_1.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chromit_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Chromite" title="Chromite">Chromite</a> ore</div></div></div>
<p>Chromium is the 13th most <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_elements_in_Earth%27s_crust" title="Abundance of elements in Earth's crust">abundant element in Earth's crust</a> with an average concentration of 100 ppm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley-25">[24]</a></sup> Chromium compounds are found in the environment from the <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">erosion</a> of chromium-containing rocks, and can be redistributed by volcanic eruptions. Typical background concentrations of chromium in environmental media are: atmosphere &lt;10 ng m<sup>−3</sup>; soil &lt;500 mg kg<sup>−1</sup>; vegetation &lt;0.5 mg kg<sup>−1</sup>; freshwater &lt;10 ug L<sup>−1</sup>; seawater &lt;1 ug L<sup>−1</sup>; sediment &lt;80 mg kg<sup>−1</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rieuwerts_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rieuwerts-26">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chromium is mined as <a href="/wiki/Chromite" title="Chromite">chromite</a> (FeCr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>) ore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NRC_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-NRC-27">[26]</a></sup> About two-fifths of the chromite ores and concentrates in the world are produced in South Africa, about a third in <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trump-SoHo-Bayrock_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Trump-SoHo-Bayrock-28">[27]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> are also substantial producers. Untapped chromite deposits are plentiful, but geographically concentrated in Kazakhstan and southern <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS2015Yb_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGS2015Yb-29">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although rare, deposits of <a href="/wiki/Native_metal" title="Native metal">native</a> chromium exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Udachnaya_Pipe" title="Udachnaya Pipe">Udachnaya Pipe</a> in Russia produces samples of the native metal. This mine is a <a href="/wiki/Kimberlite" title="Kimberlite">kimberlite</a> pipe, rich in <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">diamonds</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">reducing environment</a> helped produce both elemental chromium and <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">diamond</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>The relation between Cr(III) and Cr(VI) strongly depends on <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidative" title="Oxidative">oxidative</a> properties of the location. In most cases, Cr(III) is the dominating species,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crspeci_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Crspeci-13">[13]</a></sup> but in some areas, the ground water can contain up to 39 µg/liter of total chromium of which 30 µg/liter is Cr(VI).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:left;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_uses">Ancient uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Ancient uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Chromium was first discovered as an element after it came to the attention of the Western world in the red crystalline mineral <a href="/wiki/Crocoite" title="Crocoite">crocoite</a> (which is <a href="/wiki/Lead(II)_chromate" title="Lead(II) chromate">lead(II) chromate</a>). This mineral was discovered in 1761 and was initially used as a <a href="/wiki/Pigment" title="Pigment">pigment</a>; the distinctive color was attributed to the chromium from within the crocoite. In present day, nearly all chromium is commercially extracted from the only viable ore for extensiveness and predicted long term use, being <a href="/wiki/Chromite" title="Chromite">chromite</a>, which is iron chromium oxide (FeCr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>); chromite is now the principal source of chromium for use in pigments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Terracotta_Army_weapons">Terracotta Army weapons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Terracotta Army weapons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Weapons found in burial pits dating from the late 3rd century B.C. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qin_Dynasty" title="Qin Dynasty">Qin Dynasty</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Terracotta_Army" title="Terracotta Army">Terracotta Army</a> near <a href="/wiki/Xi%27an" title="Xi'an">Xi'an</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, have been analyzed by archaeologists. Although these weapons were presumably buried more than two millennia ago, the ancient <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a> tips of both the swords and <a href="/wiki/Crossbow" title="Crossbow">crossbow</a> bolts found at the site showed unexpectedly little corrosion, possibly because the bronze was deliberately coated with a thin layer of chromium oxide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup> Still, this oxide layer that was found on the weapons was not pure chromium metal or chrome plating as it is commonly produced today, but a mere 10-15 <a href="/wiki/Micrometer" title="Micrometer">μm</a> layer of chromium oxide molecules at up to 2% chromium was discovered, which turned out to be enough to protect the bronze from corroding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lorch2016_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lorch2016-36">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chromium_as_pigment">Chromium as pigment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Chromium as pigment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Chromium minerals as pigments came to the attention of the west in the 18th century. On 26 July 1761, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Gottlob_Lehmann_(scientist)" title="Johann Gottlob Lehmann (scientist)">Johann Gottlob Lehmann</a> found an orange-red mineral in the <a href="/wiki/Beryozovskoye_deposit" title="Beryozovskoye deposit">Beryozovskoye mines</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Ural_Mountains" title="Ural Mountains">Ural Mountains</a> which he named <i>Siberian red lead</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meyer_1962_p._37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meyer_1962_p.-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup> Though misidentified as a <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a> compound with <a href="/wiki/Selenium" title="Selenium">selenium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> components, the mineral was in fact <a href="/wiki/Crocoite" title="Crocoite">crocoite</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Lead(II)_chromate" title="Lead(II) chromate">lead(II) chromate</a>) with a formula of PbCrO<sub>4</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChromiumVI_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-ChromiumVI-39">[38]</a></sup> In 1770, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Simon_Pallas" title="Peter Simon Pallas">Peter Simon Pallas</a> visited the same site as Lehmann and found a red lead mineral that was discovered to possess useful properties as a <a href="/wiki/Pigment" title="Pigment">pigment</a> in <a href="/wiki/Paint" title="Paint">paints</a>. After Pallas, the use of Siberian red lead as a paint pigment began to develop rapidly throughout the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weeks1932_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weeks1932-40">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cut_Ruby.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="336" data-file-width="380" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Cut_Ruby.jpg/170px-Cut_Ruby.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Cut_Ruby.jpg/255px-Cut_Ruby.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Cut_Ruby.jpg/340px-Cut_Ruby.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cut_Ruby.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The red color of rubies is from a trace amount of chromium.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1794, <a href="/wiki/Louis_Nicolas_Vauquelin" title="Louis Nicolas Vauquelin">Louis Nicolas Vauquelin</a> received samples of crocoite <a href="/wiki/Ore" title="Ore">ore</a>. He produced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chromium(VI)_oxide" title="Chromium(VI) oxide">chromium trioxide</a> (CrO<sub>3</sub>) by mixing crocoite with <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChromiumVI_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-ChromiumVI-39">[38]</a></sup> In 1797, Vauquelin discovered that he could isolate metallic chromium by heating the oxide in a charcoal oven, for which he is credited as the one who truly discovered the element.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup> Vauquelin was also able to detect traces of chromium in precious <a href="/wiki/Gemstone" title="Gemstone">gemstones</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Ruby" title="Ruby">ruby</a> or <a href="/wiki/Emerald" title="Emerald">emerald</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChromiumVI_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-ChromiumVI-39">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the 19th century, chromium was primarily used not only as a component of paints, but in <a href="/wiki/Tanning_(leather)" title="Tanning (leather)">tanning</a> salts as well. For quite some time, the crocoite found in Russia was the main source for such tanning materials. In 1827, a larger chromite deposit was discovered near <a href="/wiki/Baltimore" title="Baltimore">Baltimore</a>, United States, which quickly met the demand for tanning salts much more adequately than the crocoite that had been used previously. This made the United States the largest producer of chromium products until the year 1848, when larger deposits of chromite were uncovered near the city of <a href="/wiki/Bursa" title="Bursa">Bursa</a>, Turkey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NRC_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-NRC-27">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chromium is also famous for its reflective, metallic luster when polished. It is used as a protective and decorative coating on car parts, plumbing fixtures, furniture parts and many other items, usually applied by <a href="/wiki/Electroplating" title="Electroplating">electroplating</a>. Chromium was used for electroplating as early as 1848, but this use only became widespread with the development of an improved process in 1924.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crplating_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crplating-44">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chrom_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="274" data-file-width="417" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Chrom_1.jpg/220px-Chrom_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Chrom_1.jpg/330px-Chrom_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cf/Chrom_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chrom_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Piece of chromium produced with <a href="/wiki/Aluminothermic_reaction" title="Aluminothermic reaction">aluminothermic reaction</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chromium_-_world_production_trend.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="520" data-file-width="750" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Chromium_-_world_production_trend.svg/310px-Chromium_-_world_production_trend.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Chromium_-_world_production_trend.svg/465px-Chromium_-_world_production_trend.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Chromium_-_world_production_trend.svg/620px-Chromium_-_world_production_trend.svg.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chromium_-_world_production_trend.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>World production trend of chromium</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chromium_zone_refined_and_1cm3_cube.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3060" data-file-width="4752" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Chromium_zone_refined_and_1cm3_cube.jpg/220px-Chromium_zone_refined_and_1cm3_cube.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Chromium_zone_refined_and_1cm3_cube.jpg/330px-Chromium_zone_refined_and_1cm3_cube.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Chromium_zone_refined_and_1cm3_cube.jpg/440px-Chromium_zone_refined_and_1cm3_cube.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chromium_zone_refined_and_1cm3_cube.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chromium, remelted in a horizontal arc zone-refiner, showing large visible crystal grains</div></div></div>
<p>Approximately 28.8 million metric tons (Mt) of marketable chromite ore was produced in 2013, and converted into 7.5 Mt of ferrochromium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS2015Yb_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-USGS2015Yb-29">[28]</a></sup> According to John F. Papp, writing for the USGS, "Ferrochromium is the leading end use of chromite ore, [and] stainless steel is the leading end use of ferrochromium."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS2015Yb_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-USGS2015Yb-29">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>The largest producers of chromium ore in 2013 have been South Africa (48%), Kazakhstan (13%), Turkey (11%), India (10%) with several other countries producing the rest of about 18% of the world production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS2015Yb_29-3"><a href="#cite_note-USGS2015Yb-29">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>The two main products of chromium ore refining are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferrochromium" title="Ferrochromium">ferrochromium</a> and metallic chromium. For those products the ore smelter process differs considerably. For the production of ferrochromium, the chromite ore (FeCr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>) is reduced in large scale in <a href="/wiki/Electric_arc_furnace" title="Electric arc furnace">electric arc furnace</a> or in smaller smelters with either <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a> or <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a> in an <a href="/wiki/Aluminothermic_reaction" title="Aluminothermic reaction">aluminothermic reaction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IndMin_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-IndMin-45">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_Chromium_Production_2002.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="415" data-file-width="940" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/World_Chromium_Production_2002.svg/310px-World_Chromium_Production_2002.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/World_Chromium_Production_2002.svg/465px-World_Chromium_Production_2002.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/World_Chromium_Production_2002.svg/620px-World_Chromium_Production_2002.svg.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:World_Chromium_Production_2002.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chromium ore output in 2002<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS2002Yb_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGS2002Yb-46">[45]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>For the production of pure chromium, the iron must be separated from the chromium in a two step roasting and leaching process. The chromite ore is heated with a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">calcium carbonate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sodium_carbonate" title="Sodium carbonate">sodium carbonate</a> in the presence of air. The chromium is oxidized to the hexavalent form, while the iron forms the stable Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>. The subsequent leaching at higher elevated temperatures dissolves the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chromate" title="Chromate">chromates</a> and leaves the insoluble iron oxide. The chromate is converted by sulfuric acid into the dichromate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IndMin_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-IndMin-45">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>4 FeCr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> + 8 Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> + 7 O<sub>2</sub> → 8 Na<sub>2</sub>CrO<sub>4</sub> + 2 Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> + 8 CO<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<dl><dd>2 Na<sub>2</sub>CrO<sub>4</sub> + H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> → Na<sub>2</sub>Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> + Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> + H<sub>2</sub>O</dd></dl>
<p>The dichromate is converted to the chromium(III) oxide by reduction with carbon and then reduced in an aluminothermic reaction to chromium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IndMin_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-IndMin-45">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>Na<sub>2</sub>Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> + 2 C → Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> + Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> + CO</dd>
<dd>Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> + 2 Al → Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> + 2 Cr</dd></dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The creation of metal alloys account for 85% of the available chromium's usage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup> The remainder of chromium is used in the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_industry" title="Chemical industry">chemical</a>, <a href="/wiki/Refractory" title="Refractory">refractory</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Foundry" title="Foundry">foundry</a> industries.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metallurgy">Metallurgy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Metallurgy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Besteck_WMF_Stockholm_ca_1960er.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="658" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Besteck_WMF_Stockholm_ca_1960er.jpg/220px-Besteck_WMF_Stockholm_ca_1960er.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Besteck_WMF_Stockholm_ca_1960er.jpg/330px-Besteck_WMF_Stockholm_ca_1960er.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Besteck_WMF_Stockholm_ca_1960er.jpg/440px-Besteck_WMF_Stockholm_ca_1960er.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Besteck_WMF_Stockholm_ca_1960er.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stainless steel cutlery made from Cromargan 18/10, containing 18% Chromium.</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Chrome_plating" title="Chrome plating">Chrome plating</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stainless_steel" title="Stainless steel">Stainless steel</a></div>
<p>The strengthening effect of forming stable metal carbides at the grain boundaries and the strong increase in corrosion resistance made chromium an important alloying material for steel. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_speed_steel" title="High speed steel">high-speed tool steels</a> contain between 3 and 5% chromium. <a href="/wiki/Stainless_steel" title="Stainless steel">Stainless steel</a>, the primary corrosion-resistant metal alloy, is formed when chromium is introduced to <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> in sufficient concentrations, usually where the chromium concentration is above 11%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davis_2000_p._48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davis_2000_p.-48">[47]</a></sup> For stainless steel's formation, ferrochromium is added to the molten iron. Also, nickel-based alloys increase in strength due to the formation of discrete, stable metal carbide particles at the grain boundaries. For example, <a href="/wiki/Inconel" title="Inconel">Inconel</a> 718 contains 18.6% chromium. Because of the excellent high-temperature properties of these nickel <a href="/wiki/Superalloy" title="Superalloy">superalloys</a>, they are used in <a href="/wiki/Jet_engine" title="Jet engine">jet engines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gas_turbine" title="Gas turbine">gas turbines</a> in lieu of common structural materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-superal_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-superal-49">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Motorcycle_Reflections_bw_edit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Motorcycle_Reflections_bw_edit.jpg/220px-Motorcycle_Reflections_bw_edit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Motorcycle_Reflections_bw_edit.jpg/330px-Motorcycle_Reflections_bw_edit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Motorcycle_Reflections_bw_edit.jpg/440px-Motorcycle_Reflections_bw_edit.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Motorcycle_Reflections_bw_edit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Decorative chrome plating on a motorcycle.</div></div></div>
<p>The relative high hardness and corrosion resistance of unalloyed chromium makes chrome a reliable metal for surface coating; it is still the most popular metal concerning sheet coating with its above average durability compared to other coating metals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup> A layer of chromium is deposited on pretreated metallic surfaces by <a href="/wiki/Electroplating" title="Electroplating">electroplating</a> techniques. There are two deposition methods: thin and thick. Thin deposition involves a layer of chromium below 1 µm thickness deposited by <a href="/wiki/Chrome_plating" title="Chrome plating">chrome plating</a>, and are used for decorative surfaces. Thicker chromium layers are deposited if wear-resistant surfaces are needed. Both methods use acidic chromate or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dichromate" title="Dichromate">dichromate</a> solutions. To prevent the energy-consuming change in oxidation state, the use of chromium(III) sulfate is under development; for most applications of chromium, the previously established process is used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crplating_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Crplating-44">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Chromate_conversion_coating" title="Chromate conversion coating">chromate conversion coating</a> process, the strong oxidative properties of chromates are used to deposit a protective oxide layer on metals like aluminium, zinc and cadmium. This <a href="/wiki/Passivation_(chemistry)" title="Passivation (chemistry)">passivation</a> and the self-healing properties by the chromate stored in the chromate conversion coating, which is able to migrate to local defects, are the benefits of this coating method.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards-51">[50]</a></sup> Because of environmental and health regulations on chromates, alternative coating methods are under development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chromic acid <a href="/wiki/Anodizing" title="Anodizing">anodizing</a> (or Type I anodizing) of aluminium is another electrochemical process, which does not lead to the deposition of chromium, but uses <a href="/wiki/Chromic_acid" title="Chromic acid">chromic acid</a> as electrolyte in the solution. During anodization, an oxide layer is formed on the aluminium. The use of chromic acid, instead of the normally used sulfuric acid, leads to a slight difference of these oxide layers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-surface_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-surface-53">[52]</a></sup>
The high toxicity of Cr(VI) compounds, used in the established chromium electroplating process, and the strengthening of safety and environmental regulations demand a search for substitutes for chromium or at least a change to less toxic chromium(III) compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crplating_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-Crplating-44">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dye_and_pigment">Dye and pigment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Dye and pigment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Laidlaw_school_bus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="700" height="94" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Laidlaw_school_bus.jpg/220px-Laidlaw_school_bus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Laidlaw_school_bus.jpg/330px-Laidlaw_school_bus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Laidlaw_school_bus.jpg/440px-Laidlaw_school_bus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Laidlaw_school_bus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>School bus painted in <a href="/wiki/Chrome_yellow" title="Chrome yellow">chrome yellow</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The mineral <a href="/wiki/Crocoite" title="Crocoite">crocoite</a> (which is also <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lead_chromate" title="Lead chromate">lead chromate</a> PbCrO<sub>4</sub>) was used as a yellow pigment shortly after its discovery. After a synthesis method became available starting from the more abundant chromite, <a href="/wiki/Chrome_yellow" title="Chrome yellow">chrome yellow</a> was, together with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cadmium_yellow" title="Cadmium yellow">cadmium yellow</a>, one of the most used yellow pigments. The pigment does not photodegrade, but it tends to darken due to the formation of chromium(III) oxide. It has a strong color, and was used for school buses in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> and for the Postal Service (for example, the <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Post" title="Deutsche Post">Deutsche Post</a>) in Europe. The use of chrome yellow has since declined due to environmental and safety concerns and was replaced by organic pigments or other alternatives that are free from lead and chromium. Other pigments that are based around chromium are, for example, the deep shade of red pigment <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chrome_red" title="Chrome red">chrome red</a>, which is simply lead chromate with <a href="/wiki/Lead(II)_hydroxide" title="Lead(II) hydroxide">lead(II) hydroxide</a> (PbCrO<sub>4</sub>·Pb(OH)<sub>2</sub>). A very important chromate pigment, which was used widely in metal primer formulations, was zinc chromate, now replaced by zinc phosphate. A wash primer was formulated to replace the dangerous practice of pre-treating aluminium aircraft bodies with a phosphoric acid solution. This used zinc tetroxychromate dispersed in a solution of <a href="/wiki/Polyvinyl_butyral" title="Polyvinyl butyral">polyvinyl butyral</a>. An 8% solution of phosphoric acid in solvent was added just before application. It was found that an easily oxidized alcohol was an essential ingredient. A thin layer of about 10–15 µm was applied, which turned from yellow to dark green when it was cured. There is still a question as to the correct mechanism. Chrome green is a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Prussian_blue" title="Prussian blue">Prussian blue</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chrome_yellow" title="Chrome yellow">chrome yellow</a>, while the chrome oxide green is <a href="/wiki/Chromium(III)_oxide" title="Chromium(III) oxide">chromium(III) oxide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cryel_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cryel-55">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chromium oxides are also used as a green pigment in the field of glassmaking and also as a glaze for ceramics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup> Green chromium oxide is extremely <a href="/wiki/Lightfastness" title="Lightfastness">lightfast</a> and as such is used in cladding coatings. It is also the main ingredient in <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> reflecting paints, used by the armed forces to paint vehicles and to give them the same infrared reflectance as green leaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Synthetic_ruby_and_the_first_laser">Synthetic ruby and the first laser</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Synthetic ruby and the first laser">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:5_Maiman_Laser_Components.jpg"><img alt="Components of original ruby laser." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="960" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/5_Maiman_Laser_Components.jpg/220px-5_Maiman_Laser_Components.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/5_Maiman_Laser_Components.jpg/330px-5_Maiman_Laser_Components.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/5_Maiman_Laser_Components.jpg/440px-5_Maiman_Laser_Components.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:5_Maiman_Laser_Components.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Red crystal of an ruby laser</div></div></div>
<p>Natural <a href="/wiki/Ruby" title="Ruby">rubies</a> are <a href="/wiki/Corundum" title="Corundum">corundum</a> (aluminum oxide) crystals that are colored red (the rarest type) due to chromium (III) ions (other colors of corundum gems are termed <a href="/wiki/Sapphire" title="Sapphire">sapphires</a>). A red-colored artificial ruby may also be achieved by doping chromium(III) into artificial corundum crystals, thus making chromium a requirement for making synthetic rubies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup> Such a synthetic ruby crystal was the basis for the first <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">laser</a>, produced in 1960, which relied on <a href="/wiki/Stimulated_emission" title="Stimulated emission">stimulated emission</a> of light from the chromium atoms in such a crystal. A ruby laser is lasing at 694.3 nanometers, in a deep red color.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WebbJones2004_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-WebbJones2004-59">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wood_preservative">Wood preservative</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Wood preservative">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Because of their toxicity, chromium(VI) salts are used for the preservation of wood. For example, <a href="/wiki/Chromated_copper_arsenate" title="Chromated copper arsenate">chromated copper arsenate</a> (CCA) is used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timber_treatment" title="Timber treatment">timber treatment</a> to protect wood from decay fungi, wood-attacking insects, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Termites" title="Termites">termites</a>, and marine borers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hings_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hings-60">[59]</a></sup> The formulations contain chromium based on the oxide CrO<sub>3</sub> between 35.3% and 65.5%. In the United States, 65,300 metric tons of CCA solution were used in 1996.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hings_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hings-60">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tanning">Tanning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Tanning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tanning_(leather)" title="Tanning (leather)">Tanning (leather)</a></div>
<p>Chromium(III) salts, especially <a href="/wiki/Chrome_alum" title="Chrome alum">chrome alum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chromium(III)_sulfate" title="Chromium(III) sulfate">chromium(III) sulfate</a>, are used in the tanning of <a href="/wiki/Leather" title="Leather">leather</a>. The chromium(III) stabilizes the leather by cross linking the <a href="/wiki/Collagen" title="Collagen">collagen</a> fibers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup> Chromium tanned leather can contain between 4 and 5% of chromium, which is tightly bound to the proteins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NRC_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-NRC-27">[26]</a></sup> Although the form of chromium used for tanning is not the toxic hexavalent variety, there remains interest in management of chromium in the tanning industry such as recovery and reuse, direct/indirect recycling,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup> use of less chromium or "chrome-less" tanning are practiced to better manage chromium in tanning.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Refractory_material">Refractory material</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Refractory material">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The high heat resistivity and high melting point makes <a href="/wiki/Chromite" title="Chromite">chromite</a> and chromium(III) oxide a material for high temperature refractory applications, like <a href="/wiki/Blast_furnace" title="Blast furnace">blast furnaces</a>, cement <a href="/wiki/Kiln" title="Kiln">kilns</a>, molds for the firing of <a href="/wiki/Brick" title="Brick">bricks</a> and as foundry sands for the <a href="/wiki/Casting_(metalworking)" title="Casting (metalworking)">casting</a> of metals. In these applications, the refractory materials are made from mixtures of chromite and magnesite. The use is declining because of the environmental regulations due to the possibility of the formation of chromium(VI).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IndMin_45-3"><a href="#cite_note-IndMin-45">[44]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnhart1997_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barnhart1997-63">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
